In terms of the charging process, RMs are oxidized at the electrode and diffuse into the electrolyte (RM ⇌ RM+ + e−); the oxidized state of RM (RM+) in turn oxidizes the solid Li2O2 on the electrode, leading to O2 evolution and regeneration of RM:2RM+ + Li2O2 ⇌ 2RM + 2Li+ + O2.
In such a process, RM acts as a molecular electron–hole transfer agent, helping electrons pass through the insulating Li2O2.246,277 Consequently, the charging potential can be significantly reduced, thus promoting the rechargeability of the cell and increasing the energy efficiency.246
Based on the above discussion, the addition of RM to the electrolyte is a promising way to promote battery performance. Several requirements should be fulfilled for RMs in the LOBs and LABs. The selection of RMs should start with the consideration of redox potential. RMs can be divided into discharge mediators and charge mediators according to the reaction mechanisms. The redox potential of RMs is the natural selection criteria (Fig. 15b). The redox potentials of discharge RMs should be close to the equilibrium potential of the O2/Li2O2 redox pair while enabling the quick reduction of superoxide to peroxide.278 As for charge mediators, the oxidation potential should be moderately higher than the theoretical decomposition of Li2O2 (2.96 V vs. Li/Li+).279 The oxidation potential of the charge mediator should be lower than the oxidation potential of the target products so that the charge mediator is oxidized before the electrochemical oxidation of Li2O2. At the same time, the redox potential of the charge mediator should be as low as possible to minimize the charge potential so that the degradation reactions induced by the high potential can be avoided.261
The RM should be highly dissolvable in the electrolyte and not react with electrolyte solvents or the Li metal anode.282 RM should be compatible with other battery components to avoid parasitic reactions. Moreover, the chemical stability of the charge RM and its oxidized form against the reactive oxygen species should be considered as a primary selection criterion.
Since the mediation process involves the shuttling of RM between the target reactant and cathode, the mass transport of RM should not be ignored. Higher diffusion ecoefficiency enables more contact area with electrode space, thus promoting a more homogeneous reaction. However, the shuttle effect would be encouraged by high diffusivity, which hinders the energy efficiency of the battery and degrades the cycle performance of the cell.271
Besides the electrocatalytic properties, some RMs also can promote oxygen solubility.283 These RMs are FePc,284 Heme,285 PTIO,286 2,6-di-tert-butyl-hydroxytoluene (BHT),287 and RuBr3. Thus, the formation of Li2O2 can be stabilized by the resultant solvated complexing molecules, while the decomposition of Li2O2 can be facilitated by the oxidated RMs, achieving excellent cycle performance. Similarly, 6-ethoxy-2,2,4-trimethylquinoline (EQ) could solvate Li+, facilitating the formation of solution-grown Li2O2. The EQ/EQ+ redox couple could effectively catalyze the decomposition of Li2O2 at low charge overpotential, thus enlarging the discharge capacity and reducing the charge polarization simultaneously.288
Moreover, the anions and cations in dissolved RMs can independently work as the RM for the discharge and charge process. Pang et al. proposed 1,10-diheptyl-4,40-bipyridinium (heptyl viologen) dibromide (HeptVBr2) as a bifunctional RM.289 The dissolved HeptV2+ and Br− worked as the discharge mediator and charge mediator, respectively, promoting the formation of Li2O2 and lowering the charging potential. As a result, the overall potential gap between charge and discharge was reduced with no Li2O2 residue after discharge.
Early study of RMs in LABs. 
The concept of RM was first proposed in the 1980s with the use of LiBr and LiI for overcharge protection in LIBs.290,291 Inspired by these pioneer studies, Chase et al. applied RM in rechargeable metal–air batteries in 2012, laying the foundation for future application in LOBs.292 RMs for LOBs were first reported by Bruce et al. in 2013, with the addition of tetrathiafulvalene (TTF) in the DMSO solvent for the charge reaction.277 During charging, TTF is oxidized to TTF+, which subsequently oxidizes the insulating solid Li2O2 and converts to TTF. In the practical battery tests, the cell containing TTF presented a very modest increase in charging potential and significantly improved rechargeability compared to the cell without TFF (Fig. 16a1). The cycle life could last for 100 cycles (Fig. 16a2) with complete reversibility of Li2O2 formation/decomposition on each cycle as evidenced by FTIR and in situ SERS data (Fig. 16a3–a4).277
 Fig. 16  The typical RMs in the early study of LABs. (a) Tetrathiafulvalene (TTF): (a1) first-cycle discharge/charge curves with and without TTF. (a2) Cycling stability of LOB with TTF. (a3) FTIR and (a4) in situ SERS spectra for 1, 20, and 100 cycles. Reprinted with permission from ref. 277. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. (b) 2,2,6,6-Tetramethylpiperidinyloxyl (TEMPO): (b1) proposed catalytic cycle for the electrochemical charging mediated by TEMPO. (b2) DEMS analysis of the evolved gases during the charge of the LOB with 10 mM TEMP. Reprinted with permission from ref. 280. Copyright the Owner Societies. (c) Nitroxides: (c1) chemical structures of the investigated nitroxides. (c2) CV curves of 10 mM TEMPO 4, 10 mM 4-methoxy-TEMPO 5, 10 mM AZADO 6, 10 mM 1-Me-AZADO 7, 10 mM TMAO 8 and (c3) 10 mM 2,2,5-trimethyl-4-phenyl-3-azahexane-3-nitroxide 9, 10 mM tert-amyl-tert-butyl nitroxide 10 in 1 M LiTFSI/diglyme. (c4) The first cycle of LOB cells with and without 1-Me-AZADO 7. Reprinted with permission from ref. 281. Copyright 1988 The Electrochemical Society. (d) Iron-phthalocyanine (FePc): (d1) possible discharging route and (d2) charging route of FePc as the RM in LOBs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 273. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. 
Since then, various RMs have been explored for the ORR and OER in LOBs.246 In 2014, Owen et al. employed ethyl viologen ditriflate (EtV(OTf)2) as the discharge mediator for LOBs in the Py14TFSI-based electrolyte.276 The cell capacity was enhanced with a lower voltage drop compared to that of the EtV2+ free-cell. Moreover, it was found that reduced EtV+ could react with O2−. Due to the shortened superoxide lifetime, the durability of LOBs could be increased. However, given its shortcomings (e.g., smaller redox potential than decomposition of Li2O2, toxicity, and slow diffusion), ethyl viologen is not optimal for RM for LOBs. In the same year, Janek et al. utilized a four-α-methyl group functionalized 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidinyloxyl (TEMPO) as a charge mediator (Fig. 16b1).280 With high electrochemical stability, fast diffusion kinetics, and an appropriate redox (∼3.7 V vs. Li+/Li), TEMPO enabled the battery to achieve a reduced charging potential (by 500 mV) and double cycle life. Moreover, it was found that the amount of released CO2, from the oxidation of decomposition products from electrolytes and cathodes, decreased with the assistance of TEMPO (Fig. 16b2). The authors further extended their investigation to other nitroxide-based molecules (Fig. 16c1).281 1-Me-AZADO was found to be the most efficient redox mediator providing charging potentials below 3.6 V vs. Li/Li+ (Fig. 16c2–c4).
Iron phthalocyanine (FePc) has been previously explored as a molecular electrocatalyst for ORR.284 In 2014, it was first adopted as an organometallic RM for LOBs.273 FePc could chemically reduce O2 and oxidize Li2O2 through redox chemical reactions. When FePc served as the discharge mediator, the O2 molecule was attached to the Fe center and then went through a two-step transformation mechanism FePc–O2 → FePc–O2− → (FePc–LiOOLi) (Fig. 16d1). Then, LiOOLi would separate from FePc and get incorporated into the crystal lattice of Li2O2. In this case, the FePc–O2 molecule serves as a shuttle of electrons from the carbon cathode to a nucleated solid Li2O2 site. As for the charging process, the (FePc–O2)− can diffuse back through the electrolyte to the carbon surface. The FeIII/FeII couple (3.55 V) in the FePc molecule allows the oxidation of Li2O2 with the regeneration of FePc and evolution of O2 (Fig. 16d2). The FePc-containing cell presented a two-times larger capacity and lowered OER overpotential of 400 mV than batteries without FePc. Later studies reported that other metallic organics such as iron porphyrin,285tertbutyl cobalt phthalocyanine (*tb-CoPc),296 binuclear cobalt phthalocyanine (bi-CoPc),297 cobalt bis(terpyridine) (Co(Terp)2),298 and N,N′-bis(salicylidene)ethylenediaminocobalt(II) (CoII-salen)299 were effective in lowering the charging potentials and prolonging the cycle life of LOBs.
LiI RMs. 
An essential and controversial RM, LiI, was first reported for LOBs by Lim et al.282 In the charging process, I− ions are initially oxidized on the electrode surface to I3− or I2. Subsequently, the oxidized reactors (I3− or I2) chemically react with Li2O2, producing Li+ and O2 gas, with a reverse reaction into the initial I− ions. Lim et al. combined LiI in the TEGDME solvent with porous carbon nanotube fibril air electrodes.282 The charging potential of the constructed LOB was below 3.3 V (Fig. 17a). A stable cyclability over 900 cycles was achieved with a capacity of 1000 mA h g−1 (Fig. 17b). The reversible formation/decomposition of Li2O2 was investigated further using TEM analysis (Fig. 17c–e). Raman spectroscopy indicates that I2 and I3− species return to the initial I− ion state upon reaction with Li2O2 during the charging process (Fig. 17f). When Li2O2 was injected into the I2-containing TEGDME solution, oxygen gas evolved (Fig. 17g), indicating a rapid reaction between the redox mediator and the Li2O2via I2 + Li2O2 → 2Li+ + 2I− + O2.
 Fig. 17  The exploration of LiI as an RM in LABs. (a) Discharge/charge profiles of CNT fibril electrodes without a catalyst, with the Pt catalyst, and with the LiI catalyst. (b) Electrochemical profiles of the LOB cell with LiI. TEM images of the air electrode using LiI after (c) the first discharge and (d) the first cycle. (e) The corresponding diffraction patterns of the electrode before cycle, post-discharge, and after the first cycle. (f) Raman spectra of the as-prepared electrolytes after the first discharge and after the first cycle. (g) Gas analysis of the evolved oxygen after injection of Li2O2 powder into 0.05 m I2 dissolved in the TEGDME solvent. The side reactions occur in the presence of LiI RMs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 282. Copyright 2017 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (h) The possible reaction mechanisms of ether solvents in LOBs containing LiI. Reprinted with permission from ref. 293. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. (i) Proposed Electrochemistry in LiI-containing LOBs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 294. Copyright 2015 Elsevier B.V. (j) The reaction rate constants for I3− oxidizing Li2O2 as a function of the equilibrium potentials of I−/I3−. Reprinted with permission from ref. 295. Copyright 2021 by Wiley-VCH GmbH. 
Kwak et al. reported that the concentration of LiI could affect the electrochemistry in LOBs.293 XRD patterns (Fig. 17h) indicated that a high concentration of LiI in the TEGDME solvent could produce LiOH as the primary discharge product. Meanwhile, electrolyte degradation was facilitated. A possible mechanism was proposed in Fig. 17i. Lithium peroxide or superoxide formed by ORR would react with organic solvent molecules via E2 elimination, resulting in the generation of LiOOH. The presence of LiI accelerates the decomposition of LiOOH to LiOH and LiOI. The resultant products would continue to react with LiOOH, leading to the generation of O2, LiI, and more LiOH accumulated on the cathode. The operando UV-visible spectroscopy study by Landa-Medrano et al. confirmed the oxidation of the discharge products by I3−.300 The side reactions on an electrolyte with the formation of OI− were also detected. They pointed out that OI− might also come from the reaction between I2 and H2O viaI2 + H2O → HOI + I− + H+
The presence of side reactions may influence the cycle life of real LOBs using LiI as a redox mediator.
However, McCloskey et al. reported different battery chemistry involving H2O and LiI.294 They found that no O2 evolution was observed through careful characterization studies during the charging process. LiOH would react with I2 or I3− to form H2O and IO3−:3016LiOH + 3I2 ⇌ 6Li+ + IO3− + 5I− + 3H2O6LiOH + 3I3− ⇌ 6Li+ + IO3− + 8I− + 3H2O.
They proposed that the low charge overpotential observed in LiI-containing LOBs stemmed from iodo-oxygen electrochemistry rather than reversible oxygen reduction and evolution (Fig. 17j).294 In 2019, Yang et al. reported that the oxidizing power of LiI RM was dependent on the solvent.302 In low DN solvents, such as DME, neither Li2O2 nor LiOH could be oxidized by the I−/I3− RM. Conversely, in a high DN solvent (such as DMA, DMSO, and 1-methylimidazole (Me-Im)), stronger solvation of Li+ and I− ions promotes the oxidizing power of I3−, enabling the effective oxidation of Li2O2 and evolution of O2. LiOH also reacted with I3− in high DN electrolytes during the charging process. The resultant IO− intermediate could either form IO3−via disproportionation or attack solvent molecules. Liu et al. further indicated that the equilibrium of water, iodide, and oxygen in their motivating work is rather complex, and the role of iodo-oxygen species on the LiOH oxidation mechanism is poorly understood.301,303 Therefore, the exact mechanism of I−/I3− and I3−/I2 redox couples toward Li2O2/LiOH oxidation is controversial and still needs further investigations.
Recently, Cao et al. investigated the correlation between the kinetics of mediated Li2O2 oxidation and the redox potential of I−/I3− redox couple by adjusting the Li+ concentration in the electrolyte. As shown in Fig. 17j, the reaction rate constant at lower potentials switches to much higher levels. A threshold appears at around 3.56 V (vs. Li/Li+), corresponding to the oxidation of Li2O2.295 The author proposed that whether I3− could oxidize Li2O2 or not depends on the position of the redox potential of I3−/I− below or above such threshold, providing another possible explanation for electrolyte-dependent oxidizing power of LiI redox couples.
LiBr RMs. 
Similar to LiI, another halide salt, LiBr, can also work as the RM for LOBs.304 The oxidation potentials for Br3−/Br− (3.48 V vs. Li/Li+) and Br3−/Br2 (vs. Li/Li+4.02 V) couples are higher than those of I3−/I− and I3−/I2,305,306 In 2016, Sun et al. used LiBr as a charge mediator in diglyme solution for LOBs.306 As shown in Fig. 18a1, similar CV curves can be observed for both LiI- and LiBr-containing cells, apart from a voltage difference in the redox peaks. The first oxidation peaks correspond to the oxidation of I− to I3− (3.25 V) and Br− to Br3− (3.78 V), while the second oxidation peaks involve the transition of I3− to I2 (3.83 V) and Br3− to Br2 (4.13 V). A better performance was achieved in the cell with diglyme electrolyte containing LiBr than the cell with LiI due to fewer side effects (Fig. 18a2). Unlike LiI, LiBr does not promote the generation of LiOH during discharge. The discharge product was only Li2O2 and could be fully decomposed at low potentials (Fig. 18a3–a4).
 Fig. 18  Study of LiBr RMs in LABs. (a1) CV curves on Pt electrodes in different electrolytes. (a2) Voltage profiles of the first cycles of LOBs with different electrolytes (the inset shows the typical toroid deposits of Li2O2 on the cathode surface after ORR in the LiBr containing solution). XRD patterns of carbon cathodes discharged to 2 V using (a3) diglyme/LiBr and (a4) diglyme/LiI based electrolyte solutions. Reprinted with permission from ref. 306. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. Voltage and gas evolution profiles during charging (b1) without and (b2) with 10 mM LiBr as a mediator. (b3) Statistics based on at least three replications. (b4) Schematic illustration of the different reaction mechanisms when charged with and without LiBr. Reprinted with permission from ref. 304. Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Theoretically, the oxidation potential of the Br3−/Br2 redox couple is higher than the potential for decomposition of Li2CO3. This means that in addition to decomposing Li2O2, LiBr also enables the oxidation of the insulating Li2CO3 by-product on the cathode, which may benefit cell reversibility. Liang and Lu revealed that LiBr could shift the charge reaction from a conventional electrochemical pathway to a chemical pathway.304 Online electrochemical mass spectrometry (OEMS) and 1H NMR showed that the O2 recovery efficiency is significantly enhanced from 0.79 to 0.86 by using LiBr (Fig. 18b1–b2), and the generation of parasitic gases (H2 and CO) associated with the side reactions was significantly reduced. They proposed that the charging process in the LiBr-containing cell bypasses the formation of highly reactive Li2−xO2 species, which would result in the evolution of H2 and CO through electrolyte decomposition (Fig. 18b3). As evidenced by the reduced onset voltage of CO2 evolution, the author proposed the mechanism of LiBr-assisted Li2CO3 oxidation (Fig. 18b4):3Br3− → Br2− + 2e−3Br2 + Li2CO3 → 3Br2− + 2Li+ + CO2+ + 0.5O29Br2 + 3Li2CO3 + 2C → 6Br3− + 6Li + 4CO2+ + CO.
As the oxidation potentials of carbonate-based side-products generally range from 3.4–4.0 V (vs. Li/Li+),60 both LiI and LiBr are believed to facilitate the decomposition of these side-products. This finding further extends the critical role of RMs in the LOB system. It should be noted that the corrosive/reactive nature of both I2 and Br2 enables them to react aggressively with the cell components, thus leading to early cell failure. Therefore, the formation of I2/Br2 should be regulated. This can be achieved by controlling the operational conditions of the cells (e.g., by limiting the capacity).306
DBBQ RMs. 
In 2016, 2,5-di-tert-butyl-1,4-benzoquinone (DBBQ) was reported to mediate the discharge reaction by Bruce et al.275 DBBQ was capable of promoting the solution growth of the Li2O2 film via the solution-phase pathway, inhibiting the direct generation of Li2O2 by the solid-phase pathway. The discharge voltage of LOBs with DBBQ in both DME and TEGDME solvents increased twice, and the specific capacities were 80 to 100 times larger than those of the DBBQ-free cells. Unlike conventional RMs that serve as the soluble electrocatalyst and redox shuttle, it was proposed that DBBQ went through the following steps during discharge:DBBQ(sol) + Li+ + e− → LiDBBQ(sol)LiDBBQ(sol) + O2 ↔ LiDBBQO2(sol)LiDBBQ(sol) → Li2O2(s) + O2(sol) + 2DBBQ(sol)LiDBBQ(sol) + LiDBBQO2(sol) → Li2O2(s) + 2DBBQ(sol).
The DBBQ is reduced at the electrode surface and coupled with Li+ to form LiDBBQ complexes. Then, LiDBBQ binds with O2 to produce the LiDBBQO2 intermediate, which disproportionates or reacts with another LiDBBQ, to form Li2O2via solution-phase growth. This DBBQ-mediated route avoids the formation of the reactive LiO2 intermediate, thus lowering the free energy and enhancing electrolyte durability.
To promote the OER and ORR processes simultaneously, two types of RMs can be combined and serve independently during the charging and discharging process.286 Wang et al. used 2,5-di-tert-butyl-p-benzoquinone (DTBBQ) for facilitating ORR and tris{4-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]phenylamine} (TMPPA) for OER in redox flow LOBs.274 Bruce et al. added DBBQ and TEMPO to the electrolyte for charge and discharge, respectively.307 The synergistic effects generated by dual RMs enable the cell to present high specific capacity and good rechargeability.
Stability of RMs. 
RMs for LOBs have grown substantially with various organic and inorganic materials being investigated in recent years.248,311–313 The Li2O2 growth pathway has not only been regulated for enhanced capacity by the incorporation of a selective mediator, but the charging potential has also been successfully reduced below 4.0 V, resulting in enhanced round-trip efficiency and suppressed undesirable side reactions. Although advances have been made, RMs for LOBs are still in infancy. Some new scientific problems that arise from RMs still need to be clarified to find the most suitable RMs for practical application.
The first issue is the stability of RMs. Some organic RMs may suffer from similar decomposition issues as the electrolyte. Amin et al. comprehensively reviewed 20 RMs via cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic cycling tests under identical conditions. The stability for tested RMs followed the order of halides > organics > organometallics. Six RMs (LiI, LiBr, TTF, tetramethyl-pphenylenediamine (TMPD), FC, and (dimethylaminomethyl)ferrocene (DAMFC)) are selected as “primary” choices based on their electrochemical performance. Further theoretical calculations indicate the possible decomposition of TTF, TMPD, FC, and DAMFC induced by 1O2 (Fig. 19a). By contrast, inorganic LiI and LiBr have been identified as not susceptible to 1O2 and demonstrated better stability than organics.308 The resistance of organic RMs toward oxidative species strongly correlates with its molecule structure. For example, the molecule structure of benzo[1,2-b:4,5-b′]dithiophene-4,8-dione (BDTD) does not have α-H, thus more stable toward the oxidative LiO2 and 1O2 than DBBQ.314
 Fig. 19  The stability assessment of RMs in the electrolyte. (a) Free energy changes and barriers for the reactions between LiI, LiBr, TMPD, TTF, FC, and DAMFC with singlet oxygen. Reprinted with permission from ref. 308. Copyright 2016 Nature Publishing Group. (b) Molecular orbital energies of RMs and TEGDME. HOMO and LUMO energies of original RMs (black bar) and first oxidized RMs (red bar). The dotted blue line represents the theoretical formation energy of Li2O2, and the redox potential of RM+ + e− → RM reactions are indicated in the yellow region, which is required to be higher than 2.96 V. The dotted black line represents the HOMO energy of TEGDME. The grey-shaded area indicates the relative instability of molecules possibly reacting with TEGDME. Reprinted with permission from ref. 309. Copyright 2016 The Royal Society of Chemistry. Schematic illustration of (c) the shuttle mechanism in LiI-containing LOBs and (b) design of a self-defense mechanism in InI3-containing LOBs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 310. Copyright 2018 The Electrochemical Society. 
Kwak et al. proposed that the inertness of the oxidized forms of RMs and the reactivity with 1O2 play the dominant role in the stability of RMs.315 They evaluated the oxidation stability of several organic RMs via in situ gas analysis and reactivity test with reactive oxygen species (1O2, O2− and Li2O2). The inertness of organic RMs (i.e., 10-isopropylphenothiazine (PPT), TEMPO) toward O2− and Li2O2 accounted for 2e−/O2 during discharging. By contrast, the reactivity of 1O2 toward some RMs would derivate the reaction beyond the ideal two-electron transfer process. Notably, the reduction of 1O2 also occurred via one-electron transfer when contacting TTF or DMPZ due to their lower redox potential than that of 1O2 reduction. The nitroxyl-radical-containing molecules, like TEMPO and AZADO, were more stable toward reactive oxygen species than others.
In addition, the instability issue may also come from the side reaction between oxidized mediators and electrolytes.316 Kang et al. suggest that whether the oxidized RM will oxidize the solvent can be judged by comparing the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) level of the oxidized RM and the HOMO level of the solvent (Fig. 19b). When the singly occupied orbital (SOMO) energy of oxidized RM is lower than the HOMO energy of solvents, the RM can react with the electrolyte, such as ferrocene (FC), trimethylaniline (TMA), and phenylpyrrolidine (PPD) in the purple box. Otherwise, the RM is stable in TEGDME solvents, such as DMPZ and TTF, in the green box.309 In general, the lower the HOMO level of the solvent, the more stable it is against solvent oxidation.
Due to their mobile nature, soluble RMs in the electrolyte could diffuse across the separator and be chemically reduced at the Li anode. Such parasitic reactions may lead to the loss of both the RM and electrical energy efficiency and even cause an internal short circuit.280,312 In 2015, Zhou et al. designed a visual beaker cell with an MWCNT cathode and a LiI RM in a DMSO-based electrolyte.310 They observed that I3− ions at the cathode would diffuse toward the metallic Li anode accompanied by the color change from yellow to light-colored. Such phenomena confirmed the parasitic reaction between I3− and Li anode (Fig. 19c).
Strategies to minimize or eliminate such parasitic shuttle are critical to ensure the effectiveness of redox mediation in LOBs. In this regard, Zhou et al. proposed the utilization of InI3 as a self-defending RM.310 The mechanism is shown in Fig. 19d. During charging, the In3+ ions in the DMSO electrolyte would first deposit on the Li anode surface, forming a thin indium layer due to the lower standard reduction potential of Li+ (−3.040 V vs. SHE) than that of In3+ (−0.340 V vs. SHE). The formed indium layer may resist attack by the soluble I3−, as the reaction between indium and iodine needs a high temperature of at least 100 °C in practice. Consequently, the InI3-containing LOBs could achieve a highly efficient and stable cycling performance.
The catalytic activity of RMs. 
Apart from the instability issue, understanding the influencing factors of RMs on the catalytic decomposition of Li2O2 is important for selecting a desirable RM for better battery performances. Kwak et al. evaluated the catalytic activity of several widely explored RMs, including PPT/PPT+ (3.88 V vs. Li/Li+), TEMPO/TEMPO+ (3.76 V vs. Li/Li+), TTF/TTF+ (3.43 V vs. Li/Li+), DMPZ/DMPZ+ (3.29 V vs. Li/Li+), and TMPD/TMPD (3.24 V vs. Li/Li+), via measuring the charging overpotential. The order of charging potentials followed PPT > TEMPO > TTF > DMPZ > TMPD, consistent with the redox potentials of these RMs, implying that the overall charging process could be determined by the electrochemical oxidation potentials of RMs.315 In another study, however, Peng et al. compared the charging potential of several RMs, which followed the order of AZADO < TEMPO < LiBr < TTF.317 Considering the higher redox potential of TEMPO than AZADO, it seems that there is no dependence of RM's redox potential on the chemical reaction kinetics for Li2O2 decomposition. Meanwhile, they showed that the single-electron-reaction RMs (e.g., TEMPO, AZADO) exhibited a more stable and lower charging potential than single-electron-reaction RMs (e.g., TTF, LiBr).317 The catalytic activity of RMs was linked to the corresponding molecular structures. The smaller steric effects endowed AZADO with higher catalytic activity, thus a lower charging overpotential.
As the LOB system is integral and complex, the actual activity and reaction mechanism of RMs would vary with the corresponding concentration, electrolytes (e.g., solvents, salts, H2O), and electrode materials, thus resulting in non-determinacy of RMs. As both stability and activity of RMs are two important factors affecting the LOBs, the selection of the most effective RMs remains a challenge. More influencing factors governing the catalytic properties of RMs need to be elaborated to provide more mechanistic insights into regulating the cycling of LOBs.

3.3 Design of robust and high-performance air cathodes
The advanced design of air cathodes plays a crucial role in enhancing the catalytic activity for ORR and OER, preventing the parasitic reactions in the carbon substrates and polymeric binders, and obtaining outstanding electrochemical performance in LABs. The air cathode could be modified to satisfy the following requirements: (i) high chemical/electrochemical stability and minimum side reactions for the wide range of potentials; (ii) high specific areas and mesoporous volumes for the sufficient storage of discharge products (Li2O2); (iii) high mass conductivity for the diffusion of O2 and Li+ ions, and high electronic conductivity for the fast reaction kinetics; and (iv) robust and high catalytic activity for Li2O2 growth. The conductive substrates and catalysts should be considered integrally to be efficient air cathodes. Based on materials engineering, the typical air cathodes, including the carbon-based and carbon-free (metal), will be discussed from their surficial properties, materials structure, and chemical compositions. It is interesting that the large overpotential drop does not necessarily secure better catalytic activity of OERs in some cases. In particular, some reported materials (i.e., Au and Pt nanoparticles) could significantly decrease the overpotential; they are in fact enhancing electrolyte decomposition instead of the OERs.150,318

3.3.1 Carbon-based air cathode. 
Defects on the carbon surface. 
Highly conductive and porous carbons materials are the preferred choice to construct the breathing-electrode due to their tunable porosity (e.g., surface area, pore size, and morphology), surface environment (e.g., oxygen functional groups and defectives), and graphitization/crystallization. The defects (e.g., intrinsic defects, heteroatoms doped defects, and edges detects) on the carbon surface are directly related to the electrode erosion and decomposition in LABs. On the one hand, the natural defects on the cathode surface are the preferential sites for Li2O2 adsorption.319 On the other hand, natural imperfections could have adverse effects on battery reactions. The superoxide radicals are highly active with the defects and form the carbonate and epoxy groups on the carbon surface, which seriously limit the reversibility and cycling ability in LABs.
There are three routes to reduce the side effects of natural defects and even improve catalytic activity through the favorable modification of the defects. First, natural defects could be minimized in the carbon with enhanced crystallinity. The carbon used in the cathode is marked by 13C, treated at 1600, 2000, and 2800 °C. The Ig/Id ratio in the Raman test increases with increasing temperature, illustrating that the carbon becomes more ordered and fewer defects are present on the carbon crystal (Fig. 20a1). As a result, the charge/discharge profiles are retained for more than 40 cycles for the carbon with enhanced crystallinity and increase slightly up to 60 cycles. While the pristine carbon cathode presents only 18 cycles and continuously increasing overpotential, failing the cells (Fig. 20a2).
 Fig. 20  The roles of the defects of carbonaceous catalysts on the electrocatalytic performance of ORR and OER. (a) The reduction of natural carbon defects through the tuning of carbon crystallinity to suppress side reactions. (a1) Raman spectra of the pristine carbon with defects and heat-treated carbon at 1600, 2000, and 2800 °C with decreasing amounts of defects, denoted as 13C, 13C_1600, 13C_2000, and 13C_2800 in the figure. (a2–a3) Charge–discharge performance and the corresponding FE-TEM images of pristine 13C (a2) and 13C_2000 (a3). Reprinted with permission from ref. 320. Copyright 2014 American Chemical Society. (b) The generation of artificial defects on the carbon surface through plasma treatment in argon to enhance the electrochemical performance of the air-electrode: (b1) verification of artificial defects on the VACNT carbon through the XPS spectra. (b2) Comparison of water contact angles of pristine VACNTs and the as-prepared defective VACNTs. (b3) Initial charge–discharge curves of the carbon cathode in the ionic liquid PP13TFSI-based electrolyte at 0.1 mA cm−2 during the voltage range of 2.0–4.5 V. Reprinted with permission from ref. 321. Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. (c) Generation of electrocatalysts (e.g., Pd film) on carbon defects to increase the utilization of the defective sites: (c1) Schematic of the cathode architecture and reaction process before and after the Pd film selectively growth on the carbon defects. (c2) Voltage profiles of pristine carbon cathode and Pd coated carbon cathode at 0.5 mA h cm−2 and 50 μA cm−2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 322. Copyright 2012 Elsevier Ltd. 
Second, artificial defects could be created on the carbon surface by advanced techniques (e.g., plasma), positively affecting the capacity and overpotential with protecting the ionic liquid electrolyte. In a study by Liu et al., argon plasma was used to treat the vertically aligned CNTs (VACNTs) to make defects on the VACNT surfaces artificially.321 XPS results show a much higher O/C ratio in the defective VACNTs than that in pristine VACNTs (Fig. 20b1). The dangling carbon bonds on the carbon surface, formed after breaking C–C covalent bonds, absorb the oxygen-containing groups. The water contact angle on the VACNT surface reduces from 140.47° in the pristine state to 43.9° under the defective conditions. That is due to the introduction of oxygen functional groups changing the carbon surface from hydrophobic to hydrophilic (Fig. 20b2). The hydrophobicity or hydrophilicity of the cathode materials could significantly influence the side reactions on the cathode/electrolyte surface. The TEGDME-based electrolyte presents fewer side reactions on the hydrophobic carbon. In contrast, the PP13TFSI-based electrolyte is stable enough to avoid the side reactions in both hydrophilic and hydrophobic carbon. Moreover, the charge/discharge profiles show the reduced overpotential in the defective VACNTs (Fig. 20b3). The battery performance could not solely be affected by the hydrophility/hydrophilicity of the carbon materials, it also depends on specific electrolyte systems. In the TEGDME-based electrolyte, the defective carbon leads to electrolyte decomposition and carbonate formation, damaging the battery life. In contrast, the passivation effect of ionic liquid electrolytes (e.g., PP13TFSI) on the defective carbon surfaces can suitably manipulate the interface between the electrode and the electrolyte. In the presence of the stable PP13TFSI electrolyte, the oxygen groups on the defective carbon surface can provide many nucleation sites for small Li2O2 particle growth, resulting in increased capacity and reduced overpotential. Besides the O-functionalization, other heteroatom doping (e.g., N,323 S,324,325 B,326,327 and P328) is an excellent way to produce defective sites and increase the positive charge density of the carbon surface. Significantly, the doped N-atoms on the carbon surface has a robust electronic affinity to adsorb O2 molecules and weaken the O–O bond, facilitating the 4-electron reaction ways for oxygen reduction.329,330
Third, immobilizing electrocatalyst nanoparticles (e.g., Pd) onto the carbon defects is also an effective way to modify the surface of the carbon materials. Lu et al. designed a Pd/C cathode architecture through impregnation chemistry to take advantage of the carbon defects, as depicted in Fig. 20c1.322 The defect-induced side reactions are expected to be primarily suppressed due to the decreasing amount of natural defects. The Pd nanoparticles in the defects work as a catalyst to lower the charge potentials and promote Li2O2 decomposition. The positive effect of this design is revealed in the charge/discharge profiles (Fig. 20c2), where the charge potential of 3.2 V in the modified carbon is much lower than that of 4.2 V in pristine carbon.
Protective layers on the carbon surface. 
The construction of protective layers on the carbon surface is another feasible strategy to increase the stability of commercial carbon materials. Moreover, both the porous carbon supporter and catalytic species are considered together to design the idea of breathing-electrode for LABs. In 2013, Lu et al. were the first to propose a nanostructured cathode concept utilizing Al2O3 coating layers and atomically deposited Pd nanoparticles on the carbon surface (Fig. 21a1).71 The protective Al2O3 layers address the electrolyte decomposition, but increase the surface resistance of the super P electrode. The Pd nanoparticles deposited on the surface have two functions: (1) increasing the electronic conductivity on the carbon surface; and (2) working as effective catalysts to promote Li2O2 formation and decomposition. The Pd-catalysed Li2O2 is expected to be nanocrystalline with good electronic conductivity. The success of this design is verified by the SEM observation (Fig. 21a2). The charge/discharge profiles reveal the synaptic effects of the Pd catalysts and Al2O3 layers, which significantly reduce the charge overpotential to 3.2 V in the modified carbon from ∼ 4.2V in the pristine super P cathode (Fig. 21a3). Interestingly, the discharge potential (ca. 2.7 V) in the super P cathode with intrinsic defect sites is similar to that in the Pd/C/Al2O3 cathode. Still, the charge potential is quite different between these two cathodes. These results further illustrate that the intrinsic defects are helpful for the generation of Li2O2 during discharge but damage the carbon during the charge, which corresponds with the above section.
 Fig. 21  The protective layers of carbonaceous materials prohibit parasite reactions. (a) Al2O3–Pd coated carbon cathode: (a1) Scheme of the novel air-electrode architecture. The Al2O3 coating layers, the coated Pd nanoparticles, and the nanocrystalline Li2O2 reduce the charge overpotential. (a2) HRTEM of the as-prepared carbon materials in the presence of Al2O3 and Pd nanoparticles on the surface. (a3) Charge–discharge profiles to 1000 mA h g−1 with respect to the Super P, Al2O3 coated Super P, Pd, and Al2O3-coated Super P. The electrolyte is LiCF3SO3-TEGDME. Reprinted with permission from ref. 71. Copyright 2013 Nature Publishing Group. (b) In2O3–RuOx coated carbon nanopaper (In2O3–RuOx@CNp): (b1) schematic illustration of the In2O3–RuOx@CNp configuration, where CNp is coated by the In2O3 film and catalytic RuOx nanoparticles. (b2) TEM image of the as-obtained materials. (b3) Cycling performance at a current density of 0.2 mA cm−2 and fixed capacity of 1000 mA h g−1. Reprinted with permission from ref. 331. Copyright 2017 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (c) Oxygen selective low-density polyethylene (LDPE) film was applied on the CNT cathode to prevent water erosion from the ambient air and stabilize RMs in the battery process. (c1) The mechanism of the LDPE-film on the air-electrode. (c2) The O2 and H2O permeability for various oxygen selective membranes. (c3) Cycling ability of the LABs with and without the LDPE film at 2000 mA g−1 in ambient air. Reprinted with permission from ref. 332. Copyright 2010 Elsevier B.V. 
Kim et al. proposed another functional composite configuration of the carbon surface, which is based on In2O3 atomic overlayers further functionalized by RuOx nanoparticles (Fig. 21b1–b2).331 The carbon fibers are synthesized as the porous structure for facile O2/Li+ transportation and free-standing structure to exclude the binder oxidation. The In2O3 layers are unreactive with the oxygen intermediates (e.g., O2−, LiO2) and exhibit a high electronic conductivity of 6.3–10 × 10−5 Ω cm. The RuOx is highly electrocatalytic for ORR and OER in reducing the reaction barriers and overpotentials. For this efficient cathode design in LABs, the maintained charge/discharge potentials are obtained up to 150 cycles (Fig. 21b3). This long cycling success suggests that the electron conductive layer with the deposited catalyst nanoparticles is essential to suppress the oxidation of electrolyte or carbon and lower the overpotentials.
Oxygen selective membrane (OSM) is efficient for cathode protection. It can rule out unwanted contaminants (e.g., water and CO2) from the gas line, retain high O2 permeability, and simultaneously suppress electrolyte evaporation. OSM is obtained as polymeric films with good stability, hydrophobic properties, and certain porosity. The nonpolar molecular structure of OSM has high permeability of nonpolar O2 molecules and prevention of polar H2O. CO2 molecules can be captured by the functional groups on the OSM surface, such as -OH, -NH2, and -CO.333 Besides, some additional agents such as silicone oil are also a good choice to increase the solubility and diffusion of the O2 molecules on the OSM. The OSM can be used outside or inside the air cathode or combined with the cathode materials in the cell configuration. In 2010, the first OSM, high viscosity silicone oil infiltrated in the porous PTFE, was applied in LABs in ambient air with 20–30% relative humidity (RH).334 This membrane allows the passing of O2 but blocks H2O. The OSM-protected LABs work for 16.3 days in 20% RH, while the bare battery only lasts for 5.5 days. In addition, various polymers (e.g., perfluoropolyether and polyaniline) are another resource as OSMs. In 2017, the low-density polyethylene (LDPE) film was employed as an OSM to reject water erosion and maintain the positive effects of RMs (LiI) (Fig. 21c1).332 Compared with the reported OSM, this LDPE film is good at selectively permeating O2 and blocking H2O (Fig. 21c2). The cycling life in the different situations is presented in Fig. 21c1, where the cells with LDPE and LiI can run for 350 cycles while the cells without the protection of LDPE only work up to around 100 cycles.
Hierarchically porous carbon (HPC). 
Hierarchically porous carbon (HPC) is a practical design strategy to integrate catalysts and carbon matrix in terms of porosity (e.g., pore size, pore-volume, and pore structure) and surface modification allowing fast reactant diffusion, high catalyst utilization, and sizeable Li2O2 storage space. The HPC is a suitable alternative to commercial carbons to fabricate the air-breathing electrode. In 2011, Zhang et al., for the first time, prepared the HPC electrode via functionalized graphene sheets (FGSs).335 The graphene sheets are functionalized with defective lattice and oxygen-containing groups. The FGSs are developed into a 3D “broken egg” structure with interconnected pore channels at micro- and nano-scales (Fig. 22a1). The “shells” of the “broken eggs” consist of nanopores and large tunnels, which is an ideal design for air-breathing. These robust tunnels enable continuous O2 and electrolyte supply into the whole electrode, superior to other commercial carbons. These nanopores on the “shells” supply triphase regions (solid–liquid–gas) for oxygen reduction. The defective lattice sites on the FGSs control the formation of nanometer-sized Li2O2 products (Fig. 22a2). The pouch-type cells are configured and tested in an ambient environment with 20% RH for the practical characterization of this unique FGS type HPC air-electrode (Fig. 22a3). A high capacity above 5000 mA h g−1 is obtained at a current density of 0.1 mA cm−2.
 Fig. 22  Hierarchically porous carbon (HPC) enhances the mass transport/storage of oxygen gas (air), electrolyte, and discharged products. (a) Functionalized graphene sheets (FGSs) as HPC air-electrodes: (a1) SEM image of the pristine state; (a2) SEM image of the discharged state; (a3) discharge curve and the image of the LAB pouch cells in the air. Reprinted with permission from ref. 335. Copyright 2016 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (b) Macroporous active carbon fibers (MACFs) as the binder-free and self-standing HPC structure: (b1) SEM image of the pristine state; (b1) and the discharged state; (b3) the cycling performance at 300 mA g−1 and 1000 mA h g−1. Reprinted with permission from ref. 336. Copyright 2017 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (c) The carbonized and activated wood (CA-wood) with the Ru nanoparticles as a “breathable” air-electrode with open and interconnected macropores. (c1) SEM image of the pristine state; (c2) and the discharged state; (c3) the charge–discharge curves at 0.5 mA cm−2, 0.2 mA cm−2, and 0.1 mA cm−2. Specifically, the schematic diagram of active mass diffusion (the insertion in c1) and Li2O2 growth in the discharge process (the insertion in c2) are presented. Reprinted with permission from ref. 337. Copyright 2011 The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
In 2016, a biomimetic technique was employed to fabricate a self-standing HPC without using a binder.336 Unlike FGS constructed “broken egg,” a hierarchical macroporous active carbon fiber (MACF) was prepared to integrate the macroporous carbon spheres (ca. 180 nm) into macroporous carbon fibers (20–50 nm in pore diameter) (Fig. 22b1). The RuO2 nanoparticles are decorated with binder-free MACFs to enhance their electrocatalytic activity. This hierarchically porous structure of MACFs benefits from the high transportation flux of O2 and Li+ and avoids discharge product aggregation (Fig. 22b2). In addition, the MACF cathode provides sufficient and uniform triphase regions to facilitate oxygen reduction and oxidation, showing a small discharge potential above 2.0 V even after 154 cycles (Fig. 22b3).
Considering that the complicated channels in previous porous carbon are insufficient for fast reactant transportation, aligned open microchannels were innovatively constructed in 2018 to improve the mass diffusion pathway (Fig. 22c1). This work uses a wood-like 3D porous carbon matrix for Ru nanoparticle accommodation.337 These open and aligned microchannels have specifically avoided the formation of Li2O2 bulk, as shown in Fig. 22c2. As presented in Fig. 22c3, the specific area capacities of the CA-wood/Ru cathodes can reach 8.58, 7.01, and 4.51 mA h cm−2 at a current density of 0.1, 0.2, and 0.5 mA cm−2, respectively. Slight growth of overpotentials of 1.03, 1.22, and 1.29 V is obtained with the increased current density. In summary, the HPC carbon is an ideal matrix to achieve: (1) high conductivity and large specific areas for Li2O2 formation and decomposition; (2) binder-free structure to hinder the side reactions; and (3) suitable catalyst loading sites for high round-trip efficiency.


3.3.2 Carbon-free air cathodes. 
Co-Based electrocatalysts. 
The instability of carbonaceous materials motivated the boom of carbon-free materials for air-electrodes. The free-standing design is conceived to fabricate the most carbon-free (metal-based) cathode by directly growing the catalyst nanoparticles on a porous current collector. This advanced structure is feasible to avoid binder/carbon erosion and enhance catalytic capability. In 2009, Nickel foam was first chosen as a cathode support to penetrate the NMP/PVDF/carbon slurry, enabling rapid oxygen diffusion on the open frameworks and high electron transfer on the low-resistance pathway.338 This work paved the way for the building of the free-standing cathode. Inspired by the requirement for low cost and high efficiency, cobalt oxide (Co3O4) nanorods were deposited on the Ni-foam to prepare the first example of a free-standing metal cathode in 2011 (Fig. 23a1–a2).339 This new air-electrode provides open porous systems for mass transportation and accessibility, abundant catalytic sites for reactants, intimate contact for Li2O2 with the catalyst, and high structural rigidity to reduce volume expansion during the charge/discharge process. Proper electrode construction facilitates cheap Co3O4 catalysts to obtain a similar charge voltage to the expensive Pt/Au catalysts. Another facile free-standing structure is designed using Co3O4@Co core–shell nanoparticles assembled in a nanosheet-like array, where the nanoparticles consist of the metal Co core and the Co3O4 shells (Fig. 23b1–b2). The XRD results verify this designed structure (Fig. 23b3). The defected Co3O4 surface offers high ORR catalytic activity while the Co metal core enhances the conductivity of the whole network.
 Fig. 23  A Co-based electrocatalytic cathode facilitates a high round-trip efficiency. (a) The Co3O4@Ni nanorod electrocatalytic cathode is the first free-standing air-electrode design: (a1) schematic reaction mechanism of the electrode materials during the LAB battery process. (a2) SEM images of the Co3O4@Ni structure. Reprinted with permission from ref. 339. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. (b) Freestanding Co3O4@Co core–shell electrode: (b1) the fabrication route and schematic illustration of the Co3O4@Co electrode during the battery process. (b2) SEM image of the Co3O4@Co materials. (b3) XRD spectra. Reprinted with permission from ref. 340. Copyright 2017 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (c) Free-standing CuCo2O4@Ni electrocatalytic cathode materials: (c1) SEM image; and (c2) XRD spectra. Reprinted with permission from ref. 341. Copyright 2018 Springer-Verlag GmbH Germany. (d) A free-standing NiCo2O4@Ni electrocatalytic cathode: (d1) SEM image; (d2) XRD spectra. Reprinted with permission from ref. 342. Copyright 2015 Springer Nature. (e) A free-standing MnCo2O4@Ni electrocatalytic cathode: (e1) HR FE-SEM image; (e2) cycling performance. Reprinted with permission from ref. 343. Copyright 2008 Electrochemical Society. 
Binary metal oxide catalysts are attractive catalysts for enhanced electron transfer, oxygen intermediates adsorption, and, ultimately, outstanding electrocatalytic performance. Among them, Co-containing binary metal oxides have been intensely studied as the free-standing form. CuCo2O4 is synthesized as free-standing nanowires on nickel foam (Fig. 23c1–c2).341 HR-SEM images exhibit a nanowire interlaced umbrella-like structure. The free-standing CuCo2O4 cathode is suitable for electronic conductivity. It tolerates the volume expansion during the battery process. The experiments delivered long cycling stability up to 146 cycles with 91.1% capacity retention at 0.1 mA cm−2 and 500 mA h g−1. NiCo2O4, which is fabricated as a hierarchically carbon-free structure, is also a superior catalyst for the oxygen reduction reaction (Fig. 23d1–d2).342 The SEM image shows the scale-like NiCo2O4 with a thickness of 60–100 nm. XRD patterns show prominent NiCo2O4 peaks. MnCo2O4, which is the partial substitution of Co atoms with Mn atoms in CoOx, is prepared to increase the catalytic ability of Co sites.344 Park et al. synthesized a free-standing MnCo2O4 nanoneedle array on the Ni matrix as the oxygen electrode (Fig. 23e1–e2).343 The nanoneedle array enabled facile Li2O2 deposition. The two pairs of redox couples, including Mn2+/Mn3+ and Co2+/Co3+, provide oxygen vacancies on the catalyst surface, benefiting oxygen adsorption during the oxygen reduction reactions. As a result, this MnCo2O4@Ni cathode shows a high specific capacity of 10000 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1, a low initial charging potential of around 3.5 V at 7000 mA h g−1 and a long cycling time of 120 cycles at 1000 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1.
Ti-Based electrocatalysts. 
Ti-Based catalysts, a vast family containing oxides, nitrides, and carbides, are feasible cathode candidates due to their sufficient electronic conductivity and excellent stability to resist nucleophilic attack. TiO2 is famous for its high chemical/electrochemical stability in harsh environments, but it produces poor catalytic performance because of its low electronic conductivity. Modified by oxygen deficiencies on the surface, the white TiO2 could transform into black TiO2. The as-prepared black TiO2 has numerous Ti3+ ions and oxygen vacancies, triggering high electronic conductivity and electrocatalytic activity.348,349 Park et al. designed a carbon and binder-free electrode through the integration of black TiO2 with an ordered macro-/mesoporous architecture (Fig. 24a1).345 This structured TiO2 has high electronic conductivity, interconnected micrometer-sized channels, and a mesoporous surface, which provide a fast diffusion pathway for various masses (e.g., electrons, ions, oxygens, and LiI RMs). A high specific capacity of ∼7500 mA h g−1 is obtained at 500 mA g−1 and 2.2–4.5 V (Fig. 24a2). As a result, the toroid-like Li2O2 regularly grows and stacks on the highly ordered TiO2 architecture with increasing discharge capacity, which is visibly witnessed in Fig. 24a3–a6. Ti4O7, another form of titanium oxide with oxygen vacancies and Ti3+ ions in the crystalline lattice, has excellent electrical conductivity.350 To improve the specific capacity, Magneli’ phase Ti4O7 (M-Ti4O7) nanospheres are prepared and deposited with a highly active catalyst RuO2 to form bifunctional RuO2@M-Ti4O7 cathodes. The as-prepared RuO2@M-Ti4O7 delivers a low overpotential of 0.9 V, significantly lower than that of common TiO2 nanospheres (1.91 V) and M-Ti4O7 nanospheres (1.53 V).
 Fig. 24  Ti-based catalysts for superior energy density and stability during electrocatalytic reactions. (a) Black TiO2@Ni mesh with a hierarchically-ordered-porous structure: (a1) SEM images of the as-prepared materials; (a2–a6) the voltage profiles of the black TiO2 and the complementary scheme of Li2O2 growth in the ordered electrode architecture. Reprinted with permission from ref. 345. Copyright 2015 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (b) RuOx/TiN nanotube arrays: (b1) reaction mechanism to facilitate the O2 reactions and suppress the generation of Li2CO3. (b2) Voltage profiles of the LOBs with TiN, RuOx/TiN, and RuOx/super P cathode. (b3–b4) Electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) test of the related gases at the (b3) discharge and (b4) charge of LOBs using the RuOx/TiN air cathode. Reprinted with permission from ref. 346. Copyright 2013 Springer Nature. (c) TiC electrode nanoparticles: (c1) SEM image. (c2–c3) Electrochemical performance of TiC cathodes in DMSO solvent. (c4) PXRD patterns during cycling. Reprinted with permission from ref. 347. Copyright 2015 Springer Nature. 
Titanium nitride (TiN) has attracted extensive attention as the electrocatalyst for ORR. However, TiN nanoparticles have poor capacities and severe polarization. For improving their utilization, the self-standing TiN nanotube arrays (TiN-NTA) are electrodeposited with RuOx on its interface, called RuOx/TiN NTA (Fig. 24b1). The vertically aligned NTA offers a high electron transfer rate through the walls of the nanotubes and fast oxygen diffusion from the vertical channels. RuOx can facilitate the OER process in aprotic LOBs. As a result, the charge voltage in the RuOx/TiN NTA electrode is lower than that in the TiN NTA electrode and the RuOx electrodes (Fig. 24b2). Differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) is applied to analyze the discharge and charge process of ORR and OER (Fig. 24b3–b4) in an O2/Ar flow. During discharge, O2 is reduced below 2.9V, illustrating the formation of Li2O2. No CO2 or H2 peaks are detected in this process. During charge, the Li2O2 oxidation accelerates below 3.5 V. The steady O2 consumption in ORR, but vibrant O2 consumption peaks in OER illustrate the different routes of Li2O2 formation and decomposition during discharge and charge. A trace amount of CO2 is detected at the end of charge profiles, showing electrolyte decomposition and the formation of carbonates.
Titanium carbide (TiC) is the most promising catalyst in advanced cathodes due to its lightweight, low cost, ease of handling, high stability, and high specific capacity. In 2013, Bruce et al. constructed a stable TiC-based cathode for aprotic LOBs, which significantly suppressed the side reactions from the electrolyte and electrode decay compared with carbon electrodes. It also presents better Li2O2 formation/decomposition with high reversibility than nanoporous Au electrodes (Fig. 24c1). In DMSO electrolyte, the TiC-based cathode delivers a discharge potential of 2.7 V and a specific capacity of 360 mA h g−1 (calculated from the total mass of the electrode) (Fig. 24c2). Furthermore, around 98% capacity is retained after 100 cycles in the TiC cathode compared to 95% for the nanoporous Au electrode under the same testing conditions (Fig. 24c3). PXRD results display the reversible formation and decomposition of discharge products (Li2O2) in DMSO electrolytes.
Novel nanostructured electrocatalysts. 
Besides these noble metal and transition metal oxides, some novel nanostructured catalysts are cultivated with specific properties to enhance ORR and OER kinetics. The perovskite-based electrocatalysts, arising from the ORR catalysts in fuel cells, present high electronic/ionic conductivity and bi-functional catalytic activity in LOBs.354 The general formula of the perovskite is ABO3, where A is the alkaline earth element, B is the transition metal element, and the oxygen is coordinated with B in six numbers. CaMnO3,355 LaNiO3,356 and LaFeO3357 have been reported as effective perovskite-based catalysts with good cell performance. The ABO3 perovskite is accessible for partial cation substitution at A and B sites, generating  and  type perovskites to improve the catalytic activity for ORR and OER.358–361 In 2013, an efficient perovskite catalyst, porous nanotubes La0.75Sr0.25MnO3 (PNT-LSM), was reported for the first time with good round-trip efficiency in LOBs.352 The hollow and porous tubular structure increases the mass diffusion of reactants and the dense active catalytic sites (Fig. 25a1–a2). The initial charge–discharge profiles at 0.025 mA cm−2 show the reduced overpotential of the PNT-LSM/KB cathode compared with the KB cathode: the discharge voltage plateau in PNT-LSM is much higher about 30–50 mV than that in KB, and the charge voltage in PNT-LSM is much lower, about 200 mV, than that in KB (Fig. 25a3).
 Fig. 25  Novel nanostructured catalysts with specific properties for electrocatalytic oxygen reactions. (a) The perovskite-based electrocatalysts (e.g., La0.75Sr0.25MnO3 nanotubes, PNT-LSM) in the air-electrode for their high electronic/ionic conductivity and outstanding catalytic activity: (a1) HR-TEM images of the as-prepared PNT-LSM and (a2) schematic illustration of oxygen diffusion through the structure of the porous nanotube. (a3) Initial charge–discharge profile of Li–O2 cells with PNT/KB or KB air-electrode at 0.025 mA cm−2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 351. Copyright 2017 Elsevier Ltd. (b) Organic electrode materials (OEMs) and tubular polypyrrole (TPPY) cathode for their inherent flexibility and metal-free materials resources: (b1) TEM image of TPPy. (b2) Uniform O2 distribution across the porous TPPY cathode. (b3) Schematic illustration of the discharge/charge process on the TPPy structure. Reprinted with permission from ref. 352. Copyright 2012 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (c) MOF-based catalysts with open metal sites for enhanced O2 binding and large ordered porosity for products storage. (c1) Illustration of oxygen diffusion through MOF materials. (c2) The comparison of oxygen adsorption for various MOFs at 273 K low-pressure isotherms with the adsorption amount of 1 atm. (c3) Discharge curves of Li–O2 cells with different MOFs and super P composition in an O2 atmosphere at 50 mA g−1 at room temperature. Reprinted with permission from ref. 353. Copyright 2014 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Organic electrode materials (OEMs) appear in LOBs as the cathode because of their unique properties of high flexibility, eco-friendliness, and tunability, promoting the development of wearable LOB devices. The most widely used OEMs in air-electrodes are conductive polymers, such as polyaniline (PANI),362 polypyrroles (PPy),363 and poly(3,4-ethylene dioxythiophene) (PEDOT),364 which are electronically conductive and stable in the organic electrolyte. However, traditional OEMs have huge bulk with limited porosity. In 2012, tubular PPy (TPPy) was designed for air-electrodes, built with good conductive networks to allow a uniform oxygen gas transfer pathway through the inner hollow channels and sufficient electronic transfer through the walls of the nanotubes (Fig. 25b1–b2). However, it is still challenging to identify the stability of OEMs in contact with discharge intermediates (e.g., O2−) and discharge products (Li2O2). More efforts are needed to explore novel OEMs and other LOB cell configurations.
Metal–organic frameworks (MOFs) have emerged as a novel family of advanced catalysts in the air-electrode, featuring controlled pore size distribution, ultrahigh porosity, and easy tailorability. Most importantly, MOFs have unique advantages, including metal sites for enhanced O2 binding, high catalytic activity, and large ordered porosity for mass transport and product storage (Fig. 25c1).365 Various MOFs, such as MOF-5, HKUST-1, and M-MOF-74 (M = Co, Mg, and Mn), were investigated as air-electrodes. Compared with the low O2 adsorption in super P (0.7 mg g−1), the MOFs present a relatively high O2 uptake capability, as shown in Fig. 25c2. The discharge capacities of super P, Co-MOF-74, HKUST-1, Mg-MOF-74, and Mn-MOF-74 are 2170, 3630, 4170, 4560, and 9420 mA h g−1, respectively, when the cells are tested in 50 mA g−1. The much higher capacity in the MOF-based cathode than pure super P cathode illustrates the significant role of open metal sites and ordered porosity in MOFs for oxygen reactions. It is suggested that the electronic conductivity of the MOF-based cathode can be improved to decrease electrochemical polarization by adding conductive species or synthesizing intrinsically electronic conductive MOFs. The Mn, Ni, and Co metal atoms are the suggested central ions in MOFs, which have shown high battery performance in the examples of Mn2(DOBDC),366 Ni(4,4′-bipy)(H3BTC),367 and Co-MOF-74.368 Besides, morphological structure is another important criterion in the energy conversion process of MOFs. Downsizing the particle size is a general and effective way to accelerate the charge-transport route and enhance the activity of oxygen reactions.368 The thin 2D nanosheet is also a preferred morphology to own many open sites and high specific areas.369

3.4 Stabilization of the lithium anode
There is no doubt that the batteries in EVs have higher safety standards than gasoline vehicles. The overcharging issues or internal shorts, common in LIBs, are not present in LABs as the reactant O2 is real-time supplied and not stored in the battery. However, the lithium metal anode is well-known as one of the main safety hazards in LABs, which occurs due to dendrite aggregation and various corrosion reactions with the contaminants.

3.4.1 Challenges of lithium anodes. 
Common lithium anode problems. 
Historically, the discovery of interfacial reactions on metallic lithium anode can be ultimately traced back to lithium metal batteries (LMBs). Lewis and Keyes first reported the secondary LMB assembled using lithium metal in 1913.370 It was not until 1987 that Semkow and Sammells attempted the first account of LABs in solid-state ZrO2 electrolyte.371 The first generation of the lithium–ion battery was commercialized by Sony Co. in 1991, leading to the materials science revolution in LMBs in the following years.372 From the perspective of historical development, the explosive increase in lithium metal anode research of the antecedent LMBs laid a solid foundation for the germination of LABs.
Lithium metal has high energy density and high operation voltage compared with other anode candidates (e.g., sodium, potassium, and so on) as metallic lithium occupies a high Fermi energy level, thus presenting a relatively negative reductive potential. However, pure LMB inevitably suffers from severe degradation on the lithium metal surfaces, including rough lithium dendrites, passivated solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), and large volume changes. The significant challenges in the usage of lithium metal cause irreversible capacity fading, shorter cycle life, and low safety. Brissot dedicated ca. ten years to study the lithium metal issues in battery cycling. The in situ visualizations were applied to real-time observe the lithium dendritic growth process in a lithium/PEO-LiTFSI/lithium battery in 1998 (Fig. 26a).373–378 They found that dendrites mainly form on the surface of lithium metal under an inhomogeneous electric field. The porous and structurally open lithium dendrite layer on the anode substrate, to some extent, cannot block the accelerating corrosion by the electrolyte and loss of the lithium anode materials during electrochemical cycling. Most importantly, after the lithium dendrites continuously grow and accumulate on the anode interfaces, they crack the separator, giving rise to battery internal-short circuiting and even catastrophic fire or explosion. Another severe issue of the lithium electrode is the multiple stacking layers of side products (Fig. 26b), well known as the natural SEI passivation layer first reported by Peled in 1979.379 The electrochemically unstable lithium metal presents high activity in consuming the electrolyte, producing the isolated and dead lithium complex in the rough and fragile SEI layer. Peled persistently studied the interfacial behavior model of alkali and alkaline earth metals in different electrolyte systems for over 15 years. He fundamentally proposed that the SEI plays a prominent role as a mass diffusion barrier for electrons, salt anions, and solvent molecules between the electrode and the electrolyte.380,381 Until 1997, according to Peleds’ reports, the actual morphology and chemical composition of the SEI was still mosaic and vague.382 Later research revealed that the SEI probably consists of two phases, including an inorganic layer with LiF and Li2CO3, and an organic layer with different hydrocarbon moieties such as ROCO2Li or RCOO2Li, from the electrolyte.383–386
 Fig. 26  The challenges of the lithium anode in LABs. (a) Li dendrite with a typical arborescent-like structure at a high current density of 0.7 A cm−2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 376. All energetics are calculated at the B3LYP/6-31+G(d) level of theory with enthalpies (eV) in solution (e = 21) at 298 K. (d) CO2-Induced side reactions lead to the formation of Li2CO3, which is evidenced by the SEM image (d1) and EELS spectra (d2). Reprinted with permission from ref. 388.
