(e) RM-Induced side reactions (e.g., 10-methylphenothiazine, MPT) for the failure of a lithium anode, resulting in the production of lithium oxide (Li2O, Li2O2, green colour), lithium carbonate (Li2CO3, blue color), lithium hydroxide (LiOH, orange color), organolithium compounds (yellow color), and MPT decomposition products (violet color). Reprinted with permission from ref. 389. Copyright 2013 Nature Publishing Group. (f) H2O-induced side reactions with the generation of specific side products LiOH verified by operando XRD observation. Reprinted with permission from ref. 390. Copyright 2014 Elsevier B.V. (f1) Voltage profile of the operando Li–O2 battery during the cycling time. The curve marks of i, ii, iii, iv, v, vi, vii, viii, v, and ix are the collection points on the XRD data. (f2–f4) Three representative XRD packs were obtained from stages i, iii, and ix; each pack contains seven selected XRD patterns collected at different interfacial depths. (f5) Schematic diagram of O2 flow and cell connections from the 1st cell to the 6th cell through a single pathway. (f6) Table of the discharge capacity and the cell weight change after discharge for each cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 391. Copyright 1913 American Chemical Society. 
Before people revisited the LABs in 2006, three main mechanisms were generally put forward to explain the formation of lithium dendrites and SEI. Aurbach, in 1995, believed that the SEI layer is formed but later broken because the uneven current distribution at a specific site results in different lithium growth/deposition speeds.392 The lithium dendrites grew at the SEI broken sites with higher conductivity of lithium ions. Later, in 1999, Brissot suggested that the dendrite growth was due to the variation of ion concentration on lithium surfaces induced by local inhomogeneities or high current density.374 Later, the defect stimulation mechanism was introduced by Gireaud in 2006.393 He used homogeneous lithium deposition to achieve uniform morphology and dead dendrites on the surface, addressing the anode material drawbacks (e.g., defects sites, grain boundaries, and ridge-lines).
When considering the anode problems of LABs, which differ from previous lithium metal batteries working in closed electrochemical environments, more rigorous problems of lithium electrode corrosion should be faced mainly because of its open system with complicated contaminants (e.g., O2, N2, CO2, H2O) in the gas supply line and electrolyte. Interestingly, the lithium dendrite scenario was excluded to some extent in the LABs because external mechanical pressure on the lithium electrode positively affects the uniform plating of lithium and electrode stability.394–396
Contaminative gas and RM induced side reactions. 
As mentioned above, lessons of side reactions on lithium anode surfaces were learned from the early study of LMBs. Later, extensive investigation of the lithium anode in LABs critically examined the formation conditions and mechanism of SEI layers. As in situ/ex situ characterization technologies were introduced, it was found that the lithium metal electrode in LABs degrades mainly because of the parasitic reaction induced by the oxygen species crossover (e.g., O2, H2O, CO2) and electrolyte decomposition, causing the accumulation of insulating side products on the lithium surface.
LABs are semi-open cells in an oxygen-rich environment, where O2 inflows and reacts at the cathode. However, the oxygen inevitably dissolves into the electrolyte and passes through the separator to the lithium anode. A complex electrolyte decomposition reaction could be induced on the surface of lithium metal in the presence of oxygen. To date, this oxygen/superoxide corrosion effect has been regarded as the significant inducement of lithium anode decay in LABs (Fig. 26c). In 2013, Assary et al., for the first time, investigated the reaction of O2 crossover on the lithium electrode using an ether-based (TEGDME) electrolyte in a Li–O2 battery through DFT prediction and in situ characterization.387 The DFT calculation predicts possible electrolyte decomposition pathways under the effect of O2 and Li electrodes shown in Fig. 26c2 and the final multiple oxidative products including LiOH, Li2CO3, and lithium alkyl carbonates. At the same time, in situ high-energy XRD and FTIR were used to observe the deposition of these insoluble side products on the Li electrode during cycling. These block the Li+ migration into the anode, resulting in higher resistance and poor cycle performance. Later, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was used to study the artificial SEI formation in Li–O2 batteries. The SEI components were regulated to form crystalline or polycrystalline Li2O in electrolytes with soluble O2 or amorphous-like organic-based layers without O2.397
CO2 (0.04 V mol% in dry air) is another toxic contaminant in the LABs, undergoing parasitic reaction on the lithium anode and commonly forming Li2CO3,2Li + 0.5O2 + CO2 → Li2CO3.
According to the early study of CO2-induced impacts on the cathode side of LABs, Li2CO3 significantly improves the over-potential. It decreases energy efficiency because it is difficult to decompose during the charging process.398 In contrast, later studies reveal that the Li2CO3 film on the lithium anode surface acts as a protective layer to avoid impure gas (e.g., N2 and O2) penetration but favours lithium ion transport. Asadi et al. reported a long cycled Li–O2 battery within an air-like atmosphere where the Li2CO3/C layer artificially coats the lithium surface,388 as4Li(s) + 3CO2(g) → 2Li2CO3 + C(s).
The dense anode protection layer comprised of rod-shaped Li2CO3/C is pre-synthesized in pure CO2. The Raman spectra, XPS, and EELS (electron energy loss spectroscopy) are used to confirm the presence of Li2CO3 and carbon in the layer (Fig. 26d). In addition, the decoration of the nanosheet-like Li2CO3 protection film on the anode surface also suppresses the parasitic reaction of oxygen intermediates and H2O.399 The knowledge gained from examining the impact of Li2CO3 illuminated the generation and processing of Li–CO2 batteries where CO2 is the active material.
The trace amount of H2O crossover arising from electrolyte decomposition could diffuse into the anode surface and react with lithium metal to form an insoluble LiOH layer. In 2013, Shui et al. visually observed the accumulation of the LiOH irreversible phase on lithium anodes in both the charge and discharge processes through operando techniques including synchrotron X-ray diffraction (μ-XRD) and 3D micro-tomographic technique390 (Fig. 26f1–f4). The LiOH layers provide many voids and holes with microscopic channels, serving as a sustained lithium–ion transport pathway. The continuous consumption of metallic lithium and electrolyte in the discharge and charge process gradually increases the thickness of the LiOH layer, and finally, the battery cannot survive. Additionally, the effects of moisture contamination on the morphology and composition of the LiOH based SEI layer were later quantified by Cho et al. through SEM and XRD observation.391 The significant increase in capacity and weight following the oxygen feed lines was evaluated by the connective order of the 1st to 6th setup battery (Fig. 26f5–f6). H2O reactions at the cathode causes the increased capacity:2H2O + 2Li+ + 2e− → 2LiOH + H22H2O + Li2O2 + 2Li+ + 2e− → 4LiOH.
The excess weight on cells is attributed to the water-induced corrosive reaction on the lithium anode:2H2O + 2Li → 2LiOH + H2.
It should be mentioned that the complicated electro-active components in the air (e.g., SOx, NOx, and N2) make the anode chemistry become more intricate. With the in-depth charge–discharge reaction in the long cycle, these side reactants continuously react with lithium metal and exacerbate the corrosive effect on the anode surface. Among them, the influence of N2, with the high volume ratio of ∼78 V mol% in the Earth's atmosphere, should be considered because it is thermodynamically preferable to be reduced into Li3N products at around 1 V. Like the influence of CO2, extensive research has demonstrated the reversible N2 reaction in Li–N2 batteries via XPS, XRD, FTIR, and colorimetric methods.400 Therefore, further research on the challenges of lithium anode allows the development of LAB technology from Li–oxygen battery to Li–CO2 or Li–N2 battery and provides a theoretical basis for the proposed protection strategy.
Redox mediators (RMs) were first introduced in LABs in 2012 because they enable effective reduction of Li2O2 decomposing voltage through reaction with the intermediate charge carriers (RM+) in the air electrode (Fig. 26e). However, the soluble RM+ inevitably shuttle from cathode to anode and then continuously corrode the Li metal, leading to the loss of RMs and high overpotential. A few studies have reported the shuttle effect and decomposition of RM+ such as FePc+401 and TWMPO+402 on the lithium anode, but the fading mechanism is yet to be unexplained. In 2017, Seongmin et al. utilized MPT as RMs to specifically study the stability of lithium metal.389 They proposed that the electrochemical degradation of MPT+ damages the Li2O protection layer at the anode surface, and a thick side product layer is accumulated. Therefore, the feasible utilization of RMs in the LAB systems should be considered in further studies.


3.4.2 Protection of the lithium anode. 
Normalization of primary working conditions. 
Crucial physical factors, including operation temperature, assembly pressure, and battery testing setup, have been investigated to generalize the primary working conditions. In 2001, the battery operation temperature, especially the electrolyte temperature, was found to improve the cycling efficiency of the Li anode (Fig. 27a1).410 The Li metal precycled at a low temperature (as low as −20 °C) in LiPF6 salts mixed in carbonate electrolyte (e.g., PC, DMC, and EC) for ten cycles generally has positive effects on initial Li deposition and subsequent cycling at room temperature. Even with elevated current density, interface reactions and dendrite formation in the Li plating process are effectively reduced through the low-temperature precycling method (Fig. 27a2). Furthermore, Li deposition morphology is greatly dependent on the operated current density.411 The bare lithium surface is uneven with many defects, increasing the difficulty in maintaining homogeneous current distribution, especially in a high current density. So, despite the power density, the low current density is preferred to alleviate the current polarization on the lithium surface to increase the reversibility and stability of LABs. In addition, the high battery assembling pressure enables compact lithium deposition and uniformizes the lithium electrode surface, leading to fewer lithium dendrites (Fig. 27a3).396,412 This suppression effect has a more significant influence on the anode part of LABs than in traditional LMBs due the difficultly in applying uniform pressure on the Li anode in the closed system. So, higher inner pressure helps protect the lithium anode from dendritic lithium in LABs. Overall, the non-chemical factors provide an initial experimental basis for investigating LABs.
 Fig. 27  The protection strategies on lithium anode. (a) The working condition normalization: (a1) temperature, (a2) current density, and (a3) pressure. (b) The modification of a separator: the poreless PU separator, for enhanced electrolyte wetting and gas/water permeation. Reprinted with permission from ref. 403. Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (c) The prelithiated anode on the 3D conductive substrates, such as the lithiated 3D-CSC anode with the homogenously deposited lithium metal on the 3D-CSC scaffold, to form a smooth and stable lithiated surface. Reprinted with permission from ref. 404. Copyright 2018 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (d) The ex situ coated protective layer, such as the Al2O3-PVdF-HFP composite protective layer (CPL) coated Li electrode, to protect the Li metal electrode from attack by RMs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 402. Copyright 2016 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (e) In situ deposition of artificial SEI: as the protective layer on the Li anode surface, such as the in situ reaction of 1,4-dioxacyclohexane (DOA, e1) and PTFE molecule (157 helix type of PTFE, e2) with the lithium anode. Reprinted with permission from ref. 405–407. Copyright 2012 American Chemical Society. Copyright 2020 Wiley-VCH GmbH. Copyright 2016 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (f) Alternation by lithium metal alloy: (f1) the utilization of LixSi alloy as the anode in LOBs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 408. Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. (f2–f3) The application of LiAlx alloy in LOBs, which was pretreated at a high current density and then cycled at a low current density, resulting in the ultralong cycling performance. Reprinted with permission from ref. 409. Copyright 2001 Elsevier Science B.V. 
Separator modification for Li anode protection. 
The modified separator is a simple way to diffuse the crossover and Li-ion selectively, and most importantly, protects the Li anode from contamination by RMs, moisture, and oxygen species. For example, the poreless polyurethane (PU) separator is designed to alternate the porous polyolefin (PE) through the polymerization of the hard domain, i.e., 4,4-diphenylmethane diisocyanate (MDI), and soft domain, i.e., polytetramethylene glycol (PTMEG) and poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO), as shown in Fig. 27b.403 The MDI molecules interact through π–π and hydrogen bonding, resulting in mechanical rigidity and elasticity. The PTMEG and PEO segments provide the PU separator with good softness. The polarity of PEO endows the PU with good electrolyte wettability and a favorable Li-ion diffusion rate. As a result, the Gurley time quantitatively reveals that the PU separator is fully air-impermeable. A smaller contact angle of 9° in PU than that of 53° in PE illustrates the liquid-impermeable properties of the PU separator. The LOBs using PU separator have an additional 56 cycles than those using a PE separator, getting to 110 cycles. Furthermore, the PE separator protects the Li anode from RM erosion, extending the LOBs by >200 cycles.
3D porous scaffold for prelithiated anode. 
A three-dimensional (3D) porous scaffold is a general method to deposit lithium and build a robust lithium anode to enhance the utilization of active lithium with high specific capacity. 3D cross-stacking aligned carbon nanotubes (3D-CSC) were prelithiated and used in LOBs.404 The CNTs are extremely lightweight, ca. 0.07 mg cm−2, and own high electronic conductivity, minimizing the negative effect of the nonreactive segments. The porous and expanded scaffold enables uniform Li+ flux and less volume expansion during cycling, resulting in dendrite-free morphology with stable SEI layers (Fig. 27c). The prelithiated 3D-CSC (Li/3D-CSC) anode has a high reversible capacity of 3656 mA h g−1, approximating the theoretical value of pure Li (3861 mA h g−1). And this anode configuration exhibits 5-fold cycling performance compared with that in bare Li anodes.
Protection layers of the Li anode. 
Lithium–ion conductors, such as Li3N,413 Cu3N,414 lithium phosphorus oxynitride (LiPON),415,416 and PEOxLi-salt,413,417 are most likely coated on the lithium anode as ex situ protective layers (Fig. 27d). These protective layers usually are water-stable, having high Li-ion conductivity and outstanding stability with lithium metal. The Li3N takes advantage of the high electric conductivity of 10−3 S cm−1. LiPON has expansive electrochemical windows between 0 and 5.5 V (vs. Li/Li+) and good conductivity of around 2 μS cm−1. Another approach is to construct ionically conductive poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO) and the lithium salt mixture, namely PEOxLi–salt, as an effective polymer buffer layer to minimize the lithium problem.413,417 For example, the combination of PEO and LiTFSI with 18O/1Li, abbreviated as PEO18LiTFSI polymer composite, enables low interfacial resistance and good protection of lithium anode in both aqueous and organic electrolytes. The excellent performance is because of its inherent soft property, good chemical compatibility with lithium metal, and high ionic conductivity of 5 × 10−4 S cm−1 at 60 °C. Interestingly, the ionic conductivity of PEO can be significantly enhanced by combining ceramic nanoparticles like BaTiO3,413 Al2O3, and SiO2. Besides applying a soft polymer protective segment, Li-ion conducting glass or ceramic materials (e.g., LISICON-type and NaSICON-type) are chosen as the solid-state electrolytes (SSEs) to cover lithium metal and protect them from atmospheric pollution.
Inspired by the spontaneously formed brittle and heterogeneous SEI, in situ construction of a homogenous and highly Li-ion conductive artificial protective layer (or SEI) on the anode surface is an effective way to minimize the irreversible “dead lithium” and electrolyte consumption (Fig. 27e). Beyond the natural SEI, the artificial SEI is observed through in situ reactions at the lithium anode. For example, 1,4-dioxacyclohexane (DOA) treats the Li anode to form the poly (DOA) as an in situ protective layer on the Li metal surface (Fig. 27e1). The artificial SEI usually is incorporated with element doping to enhance the ionic/electronic conductivity. The most representative example is fluorinated SEI. Fluorinated SEI has a long history as the main practical component for minimizing lithium anode corrosion and exhaustion. Theoretically, LiF intrinsically has a large bandgap (13.6 eV), low Li+ surface diffusion barrier, and wide electrochemical stability window to assist the lithium deposition even at a high working voltage.418–420 First, the in situ electrochemical deposition of fluorinated SEI on lithium surface is considered as a reliable protective layer through the introduction of electrolyte additives such as HF or FEC. Takehara et al. reported that the trace amount of HF in the electrolyte reacts with the essential lithiated salts such as Li2CO3, LiOH, and Li2O in the native layer to form LiF.421 The dense and artificial LiF-SEI plays a critical role in smoothing the lithium anode's morphology and reducing the reactivity of the lithium electrode with organic solvents or dissolved O2.
In 2015, fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was first proposed as an effective and low-cost film-forming additive to in situ coat the LiF protective layer on the Li anode (Fig. 27e2).422 During repeated cycling, the Li metal is electrochemically treated with FEC and the in situ formed Li2CO3, LiF, polyene, and C–F species on the Li anode surface. When applying this FEC-treated protective layer on the Li anode, the Li–O2 battery delivered long-term cycling more than 100 times at 300 mA g−1 and 1000 mA h g−1. Later, LiTFSI salt had a similar decomposition reaction to form LiF-rich protection films on the Li anode surface.423 However, the electrochemically deposited fluorinated SEI is usually accompanied by solvent decomposition, leading to multiple components in the SEI with different Li plating/stripping reactions in different areas and eventual damage of the integrity of SEI. The in situ deposited SEI is thin and fragile to support superior long-term operation. Thus, LiF/F-doped carbon films were designed and controlled to obtain uniform composition, robust, and high-quality protection layers to maintain the distance between lithium metal and electrolyte for LABs.407 As shown in Fig. 27e2, a fluorine-rich carbon layer around ∼20 μm in thickness on the Li anode surface was obtained via the in situ reaction between molten lithium and PTFE.407 The C–F polar bond in the carbon surface enables it to act as Li+ adsorption sites to increase the Li+ flux and pre-homogenize the electronic structure at the anode/electrolyte surface. Consequently, F-doped carbon helps prolong the service time of anodes up to 180 cycles at 1 Ah g−1 and 0.3 A g−1 in LOBs, while the bare lithium electrode can only produce 78 cycles under the same conditions.
Alternation of lithium alloy. 
Lithium alloy is an excellent alternative for replacing pure Li anode and enhancing anode stability during cycling. Lithiated silicon-carbon nanotubes (LixSi-CNTs) are reported to provide limited lithium resources in LOBs (Fig. 27f1).408 The LOBs using the LixSi-CNT anode deliver a specific capacity of 1913 mA h g−1 in the half-cell and 1653 mA h g−1 in the full cell. The capacity of the LixSi-CNT electrode is decreased in the presence of O2, due to the excess agglomeration of Li2O2 on the cathode and the formation of LiOH on the anode. Later, LixAl alloy was demonstrated as an anode for LOBs (Fig. 27f2–f3).409 Al atoms accumulate into the lithium anode to form the LixAl alloy surface through in situ high-current pre-treatment. A stable Al2O3-containing SEI protective layer is formed on the surface of the LiAlx anode to minimize parasitic reactions and O2 crossover. In practical usage, LiAlx pretreatment is necessary. The pretreated LixAl alloy anode exhibits 667 cycles in LOBs, while pristine LixAl alloy only has 17 cycles.

4. Conclusions and perspective
4.1 Conclusions
Although tremendous effort has been devoted to the research and development of LABs, and extensive achievements in relevant reaction mechanism exploration and technological development in cathodes, anodes, and electrolytes have been made in the last two decades, LABs are at the initial technology stage in comparison to the specific capacity, power density, and stability of current LIBs. Most cell performance for aprotic LABs is usually reported at a relatively low current density and a small areal capacity (<1.0 mA h cm−2), limiting the practical application of the state-of-the-art LABs. In this review, we have presented the significant progress in the working mechanism, air-electrode, electrolyte, and anode of aprotic LABs before and after 2010, i.e., the germination period and growing period, respectively. During the germination period, the essential design criteria of air-electrodes were developed to achieve reversible LABs: (1) the mesoporous type and large pore volume of the cathode substrates are essential for mass transportation and discharge product accommodation; (2) the application of catalytic materials is necessary to reduce overpotentials and polarization of the ORR and OER; and (3) more electrolytes need to be found to replace the unstable carbonate-electrolytes. During the growing period, the most critical factors for the rechargeability, stability, and applicability of aprotic LABs, including fundamental understanding of the electrochemical mechanisms, the design of the substrates and catalysts on the O2 cathode, chemical and electrochemical stabilities of the electrolyte, and the protection of the Li electrode, are studied to generate higher capacity and long cycling life. A variety of strategies have been proposed to address these substantial challenges to aprotic LABs in this work. In addition, we would also like to extend the findings and technologies developed from LAB research to other metal–air batteries.
4.2 Perspective

4.2.1 Electrochemistry at the electrode. 
The porosity and configuration of the air cathode affect the specific capacity of the cell and the formation of the discharge product (Li2O2) on the porous O2 cathode. An effective cathode should have low electronic resistance, short mass diffusion pathways, sufficient porosity, high catalytic activity, and good stability at high potential with the activated oxygen species. Porous carbon and porous carbon-free materials are the dominant choices for the O2–cathode. For the carbon-based cathode, much focus is paid to surface engineering and nanostructured design of commercial carbon materials, including the construction of surface defects, protective layers, and hierarchically porous structure for enhanced catalytic activity, less carbon erosion, and favorable mass transportation. For the carbon-free based cathode, a variety of transition metal and metal oxides are applied to configure the porous free-standing catalytic electrode to guarantee the mass transportation of electrons/ions/oxygen and achieve a mechanically robust air-electrode with low cost. Furthermore, some novel nanostructured catalysts, such as perovskites, OEMs, and MOFs, present good electrochemical performance and extend the range of electrocatalysts. The nanostructured substrates and efficient catalysts of the air-electrode could significantly affect the specific capacity, rate capability, and cycling efficiency of aprotic LABs.
The lithium electrode has been a long-term problem in LIBs and LABs, although long-term cycling of the Li anode has been demonstrated. As previously mentioned, most LABs are still at the stage of LOBs that use pure O2 in a closed system. Significant interference from CO2 and H2O in the air (an open system) could dramatically shorten the cycling life of the LOBs. To protect the Li electrode from the crossover oxygen, controlling electrolyte reactions at the Li electrode through suitable layers or a passivation layer will be essential for achieving good performance in aprotic LABs. This functional layer should meet two criteria: (1) facilitate the rapid transport of Li+ to support current flow, and (2) avoid the reaction between O2 and lithium anode by prohibiting O2 diffusion from the O2 cathode to the lithium anode. Engineering a functional layer with a nanometer-scale thickness could be an excellent strategy to meet these criteria and address the stability of the anode. A high lithium-ion-conducting solid electrolyte interlayer between the lithium anode and the cathode could be an excellent strategy to prevent the reaction of the lithium anode with CO2 and H2O in the air. This interlayer could also protect the Li anode from shuttle reactions of a redox mediator.29


4.2.2 Further exploration of stable and functional electrolytes. 
In parallel with the importance of the electrode, LAB electrolytes (both solvents and lithium salts) play a critical role in developing an aprotic LAB cell and determining the rechargeability of LABs. As shown in Scheme 2, the development of new electrolytes stimulates the evolution of LABs. The functions and stability of the electrolyte are highly dependent on the stability of the solvents. It is well recognized that carbonate-based electrolytes could decompose in the presence of superoxide radicals. Nevertheless, despite the severe instability of carbonate-based electrolytes, many experimental results and conclusions have resulted from such an electrolyte system. However, the validity of such a conclusion needs to be re-evaluated and studied in a more stable electrolyte system, such as an ether-based electrolyte. Nevertheless, the stability of most electrolytes during the charge/discharge process, especially under high voltage, is still not precise. The current research priority is searching for an electrolyte in an oxygen-rich electrochemical environment. Therefore, more attention should focus on integrating solvents and other electrolyte components as a whole instead of solvent alone. In particular, suitable salts, functional additives, and RMs could be regarded as indispensable parts of the electrolyte system for LOBs and help overcome some flaws of the currently used solvents to some extent.
Searching for promising electrolytes is extremely challenging. Even in commercialized LIBs, side reactions in the battery cannot be avoided entirely. It is necessary to investigate how side reactions and induced side-products affect the rechargeability, performance, and life span of LABs. What's more, the reasonably integrating currently discovered electrolytes and the cathode might be a low cost and highly efficient architecture for practical applications and determination.
Solid-state electrolytes (SSEs) that present safety and electrochemical stability are recognized as a next-generation solution in LIBs, and also apply to LABs. But both these rechargeable batteries systems are equally challenging to develop. Conventional SSEs developed for LIBs are unsuitable for direct use in LABs due to the instability of SSEs towards lithium metal and/or air and the high resistance at the interfaces of the electrode materials. Nevertheless, ultrathin, high-ion-conductive lithium-ion-exchanged zeolite X (LiX) membrane is fabricated as the sole solid electrolyte for solid-state LABs. Due to the seamless integration of SSEs and electrodes, the integrated solid-state LABs delivered even better cyclic performance than LABs containing organic liquid electrolytes. This suggests that SSEs could be a promising avenue to realize actual LABs that use O2 directly from the air.29


4.2.3 Further exploration of reaction mechanisms by emerging advanced characterization technologies. 
The mechanisms involved in LABs need to be explored further to address the as-aforementioned concerns of LABs. For example, the mechanism of Li2O2 growth on the carbon electrode during cell discharge and the subsequent dissolution of Li2O2 during cell charge is still debatable. Advanced research techniques from both theoretical modeling and experimental tools are necessary to clarify these matters for developing more efficient catalysts in aprotic LABs.
Theory and computational modeling (such as DFT and MD) appear to be a powerful approach to analyze and predict battery properties (e.g., voltage, capacity, life, and so on). The well-established artificial neural network combined with the data obtained from experimental measurements and first-principles calculations might help identify the key factors of battery performance, guide the search for the most optimized electrolytes and catalysts, and modify the optimal Li–O bond formation and deformation of Li2O2. In this way, the labor and material cost for the experiments are significantly reduced, and the commercialization of LABs could be accelerated. Take the DFT in screening solvent degradation as an example, the reactions between reduced reactive oxygen species and solvent molecules, reaction-free energy, or activation energy could be calculated for possible reaction pathways involving nucleophilic or proton abstraction.63,424 The abundant evaluation methods via computational chemistry can help design stable electrolyte systems beyond the trial-and-error approach. However, the accuracy and performance of the computational methods depend heavily on the quality and scale of the database since unbalanced data would lead to underfitting problems and biased predictions.
The multiple advanced characterization techniques are also suggested to obtain comprehensive and multifaceted experimental evidence of the possible reaction mechanism. The ex situ characterization techniques, including FT-IR, Raman, XPS, NMR, XRD, SEM, TEM, and so on, have been verified to detect the valuable information efficiently. The in situ observation will play a more critical role in obtaining high-precision data of the electrode, electrolyte, and their interfaces of the LABs. With the use of the specifically-designed reactors, the in situ synchrotron X-ray technique is envisaged to insightfully detect the atomic-leveled structure information during the charge and discharge. For example, in situ high-energy X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), especially hard X-rays, have remarkable penetrating ability to study the elemental, electronic, and atomic information. The local structure information around selected atoms could be obtained from the XAS. Besides, in situ synchrotron X-ray photoelectron spectra, X-ray diffraction patterns, and X-ray Raman scattering are good choices to extract reliable and in-time data.


4.2.4 Beyond LABs to the novel metal–air batteries. 
Other metal–air batteries (MABs), including Na–air batteries, K–air batteries, Mg–air batteries, and Zinc–air batteries, share similar battery chemistry to LABs and have a much higher theoretical energy density than lithium–ion batteries.Anode: M ⇌ M+ + e−Cathode: xM+ + O2+ xe− ⇌ MxO2 (x = 1 or 2).
MABs are frequently promoted to future electrochemical energy storage for electric vehicles or grid energy storage applications. Similar to LABs, the full potential of MABs is limited by challenges associated with the metal anode, air cathode, and electrolyte.425 The research outcomes, including fundamental discovery and technological advances of LABs, could be adopted to other MABs to resolve these challenges. For example, the challenges of LABs mentioned above, such as anode stabilization, O2 crossover suppression, and CO2 management, need to be carefully addressed to make Li–air technology a practical reality. This is also applicable to non-aqueous MABs, including Na–air and K–air, which have not received as much research attention. Compared to Li–air, the lower charging overpotentials of Na–air and K–air batteries are obviously attractive for higher power density since their discharge products are mainly superoxides. Still, currently, Na–air and K–air batteries suffer from even more problematic cycle life.
Compared with other alkaline metal–air batteries, Zn–air batteries are more closely approaching practical application in ambient air due to the stable nature of anode metal Zn. The aqueous electrolytes can achieve higher safety, better ionic conductivity, less toxicity, and lower cost. However, the Zn–air batteries are limited in their lifespan due to the evaporation of electrolytes, contamination from CO2 and moisture, and the non-uniform deposition, dissolution, and corrosion of Zn. Lessons learned from the LABs can be applied in Zn–air batteries, resulting in a more reliable battery system that can be operated in the open air. For example, Zn passivation would also be suppressed by using highly concentrated alkaline solutions and electrolyte additives (e.g., SiO32−).
Furthermore, the photo-assisted Li–O2 batteries emerged as a combination of solar cells and Li–O2 batteries, undergoing the “photo-assisted charging process” in the oxygen cathode. In this kind of battery, the photovoltage of the photoelectrode compensates for the charging voltage on the oxygen electrode, leading to decreased overvoltage in the charging process. In 2014, Wu et al. were the first to design the photo-assisted Li–O2 battery by adding the triiodide/iodide (I3−/I−) redox shuttle into the electrolyte and using a dye-sensitized TiO2 photoelectrode. During the charging process, the I3− ions generated on the illuminated photoelectrode diffuse to the oxygen cathode and help to oxidize Li2O2. Thus, this device charges with a “negative” overpotential, which was thermodynamically impossible in a previous study.426 Besides, the semiconductor photoelectrode (e.g., various decorated TiO2 electrodes),427–429 metal oxides,430 metal sulfides,431 and graphitic carbon nitride432,433 also can assist the Li–O2 battery without a redox shuttle. Generally speaking, the conduction band (CB) potential of the photoelectrode should be low to reduce the charge voltage. To date, the photo-assisted Li–O2 batteries are still at the early development stage and need a lot of effort to achieve meaningful performance.

Table Content:

Zhenzhen Wu
	Dr Zhenzhen Wu is a postdoc research fellow at the University of Queensland, Australia. Dr Wu obtained her PhD degree in late 2021 at Griffith University, Australia. She was a postdoctoral research fellow at the Centre for Catalysis and Clean Energy (CCCE) at Griffith University from October 2021 to March 2022. Her research focuses on functional organic materials for battery energy storage, including organic electrode materials and organic electrolyte additives. To date, she has published over 30 papers in the clean energy field.

Yuhui Tian
	Mr Yuhui Tian received his BSc and MSc degrees from Jiangsu University, China, in 2016 and 2019, respectively. He is currently a PhD candidate under the supervision of Prof. Shanqing Zhang at Griffith University, Australia. His current research interests include the DFT-guided design and synthesis of functional materials for electrocatalysis and rechargeable metal–air batteries.

Hao Chen
	Dr Hao Chen was awarded his PhD degree in 2021 at the School of Environment and Science, Griffith University, Australia. From May 2021 to May 2022, he worked as a research fellow at the School of Mechanical, Medical and Process Engineering, Queensland University of Technology (QUT), Australia. He is now a research fellow at Griffith University. His current research interests include the development of functional binders, solid-state electrolytes, and high-voltage cathodes for next-generation lithium–ion batteries.

Tianpin Wu
	Dr Tianpin Wu is the principle beamline scientist working at beamline 9-BM of the Advanced Photon Source, Argonne National Laboratory. Her expertise is in energy storage and catalyst materials characterization by X-ray absorption spectroscopy. She received her undergraduate degree in Chemistry from the University of Science and Technology of China (USTC), and her PhD degree in Physical and Analytical Chemistry from the University of Utah. Following two and half years of postdoctoral research in the Division of Chemical Sciences and Engineering at Argonne National Laboratory, she joined the X-ray Science Division as a physicist in 2012.

Shanqing Zhang
	Prof. Shanqing Zhang obtained his PhD degree in electrochemistry in 2001 at Griffith University, Australia. He was awarded the Australia Research Council Future Fellowship for 2009–2013 and promoted to full professor and theme leader at Griffith University in 2016. Currently, Prof. Zhang is working on the design and synthesis of nanomaterials and functional polymers for energy conversion and storage devices as well as the development of sustainable recyclable battery technology.

Jun Lu
	Dr Jun Lu completed his PhD in 2009 from the Department of Metallurgical Engineering at the University of Utah. He then worked as a post-doctoral researcher under a DOE-EERE postdoctoral fellowship (Vehicles Technology Program (2011–2013)) at Argonne National Laboratory, before being promoted to a chemist at Argonne National Laboratory. Dr Lu currently holds a chair professorship at Zhejiang University of China. Dr Lu's research interests mainly focus on Li-ion battery technology, including Li–O2 batteries with both open and closed configurations, high energy cathode materials for Li-ion batteries, high energy silicon anodes, solid-state electrolytes, cathode design for Li-S batteries, and new materials for Na-ion and Na–air batteries.

2RM + 2e− → 2RM−
2RM− + 2Li+ + O2 → 2RM + Li2O2.
Pathway 1: RM− + O2 + Li+ → RM + LiO2
or: RM− + O2 → RMO2−,
Pathway 2: 2LiO2 → Li2O2 + O2
LiO2 + RM− → Li2O2 + RM
or: 2RMO2− + 2Li+ → Li2O2 + O2 + 2RM,
Pathway 3: RMO2− + RM− + 2Li+ → Li2O2 + O2 + 2RM.
2RM+ + Li2O2 ⇌ 2RM + 2Li+ + O2.
